Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thālith) for the attributes being additional to the essence. It begins by stating: if knowledge is identical to the essence, and power is also identical to the essence (as claimed by those who deny the attributes), then knowledge and power would be identical to each other—a necessarily false conclusion. The same applies to all attributes claimed to be identical to the essence. The commentator then notes that this argument is of the same type as the previous one (the second face), meaning it only proves a conceptual distinction (taghāyur al-mafhūm) between knowledge and power and between each and the essence, not a real distinction in existence (taghāyur al-ḥaqīqa). The real point of dispute is the latter, not the former. The root problem of both arguments is failing to distinguish between the concept (mafhūm) of a thing and its reality (ḥaqīqa). The passage then anticipates an objection: how can an attribute be conceived as identical to the essence when common sense sees them as distinct? The commentator replies that this objection arises from using the term 'attribute' in the philosophical sense (meaning a real accident) rather than the theological sense, where attribute includes essential properties (dhātiyyāt). The following 'قوله' lines elaborate on specific points: the correct phrasing of the tautology argument, the need to speak of concepts like 'knowledge' rather than 'knowing', and the possibility of conceiving essences without needing to stipulate predication.

The Third Argument: Mutual Identity of Attributes
Presents the third argument against attributes being identical with essence and its objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against attributes being identical with the essence, why it fails, and the crucial distinction between concept and reality.

Core Idea
Arguments based on conceptual identity miss the real question: are the realities distinct?

This lesson presents the third argument used by those who deny that attributes are distinct from the essence. The argument claims that if two attributes like knowledge and power are both identical with the essence, they would be identical with each other, which is absurd. However, as the author explains, this argument only shows a conceptual difference, not a real one. Understanding the distinction between concept and reality is key to resolving this dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then the translation. Notice the logical flow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the argument's logical structure
The argument uses the transitivity of identity: if A = B and C = B, then A = C. Here, knowledge = essence and power = essence, so knowledge = power, which is false. Therefore, attributes cannot be identical with essence.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Identify the objection
The author points out that the argument only proves that the concepts of knowledge and power are different, not that their realities are distinct. The real dispute is about realities, not concepts.
The objection is the same as for the first argument.
4
Reflect on the distinction between concept and reality
Consider why this distinction matters. The failure to distinguish between concept (mafhūm) and reality (ḥaqīqah) leads to invalid arguments.
This is a key epistemological point in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The same applies to the remaining attributes which they claimed are identical with the essence. And this argument is of the same type as the first, i.e., the preceding argument, and the objection is the same objection, meaning it proves the difference in concept between knowledge and power and their difference from the essence, not the difference in reality and their distinction from it. The point of dispute is the second, not the first. So the source of these two arguments is the failure to distinguish between the concept of a thing and its reality.

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the view that attributes are identical with the essence. The argument states that if knowledge is the essence and power is also the essence, then knowledge and power would be identical, which is clearly false. The same applies to all attributes. Then the author notes that this argument is of the same type as the previous one, and the same objection applies: it only shows that the concepts of knowledge and power are different from each other and from the essence, not that the realities are distinct. The real point of contention is whether the attributes are really distinct from the essence, not just conceptually. The failure to distinguish between concept and reality is the root of these arguments.

How Can an Attribute Be Identical with the Essence?
Explains the philosophers' view that the essence itself produces the effects of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' position that God's essence itself performs the functions of attributes, and that the distinction between essence and attributes is only conceptual, not real.

Core Idea
God's essence alone suffices; attributes are just conceptual distinctions.

This passage addresses a natural question: if attributes are distinct from essence in our experience, how can they be identical in God? The answer clarifies that the philosophers do not assert literal identity but that God's essence produces the effects of attributes, making them one in reality while distinct in our minds.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a hypothetical question followed by a detailed answer.
2
Understand the objection
The questioner argues that attributes seem distinct from essence in all beings, so claiming identity is absurd. Recognize why this objection arises from ordinary experience.
3
Grasp the answer's key claim
The answer explains that the philosophers do not mean literal identity. Rather, God's essence alone produces the effects that we associate with attributes (e.g., knowledge, power).
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Examine the examples
The example of human knowledge vs. God's knowledge: humans need an extra attribute, God does not. Similarly for power. See how the examples illustrate the point.
5
Realize the conclusion
The passage concludes that essence and attributes are united in reality but distinct in consideration (concept). This effectively negates real attributes while affirming their results from the essence.
This distinguishes the philosophers from both the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: كيف يتصور كون صفة الشيء عين حقيقته مع أن كل واحد من الموصوف والصفة يشهد بمغايرته لصاحبه وهل هذا إلا كلام مخيل لا يمكن أن يصدق به كما في سائر القضايا المخيلة التي يمتنع التصديق بها فلا حاجة بنا إلى الاستدلال على بطلانه. قلت: ليس معنى ما ذكروه أن هناك ذاتا وله صفة وهما متحدان حقيقة كما تخيلته بل معناه أن ذاته تعالى يترتب عليه ما يترتب على ذات وصفة معاً، مثلاً ذاتك ليست كافية في انكشاف الأشياء عليك بل تحتاج في ذلك إلى صفة العلم التي تقوم بك بخلاف ذاته تعالى فإنه لا يحتاج في انكشاف الأشياء وظهورها عليه إلى صفة تقوم به، بل المفهومات بأسرها منكشفة عليه لأجل ذاته تعالى فذاته بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة العلم وكذا الحال في القدرة فإن ذاته تعالى مؤثرة بذاتها لا بصفة زائدة عليها كما في ذواتنا، فهي بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة القدرة وعلى هذا تكون الذات والصفات متحدة في الحقيقة متغايرة بالاعتبار والمفهوم، ومرجعه إذا حقق إلى نفي الصفات مع حصول نتائجها وثمراتها من الذات وحدها.
If you say: How can one conceive that an attribute of a thing is identical with its essence, when each of the described and the attribute testifies to its distinction from the other? Is this not merely imaginary speech that cannot be believed, like other imaginary propositions whose truth is impossible, so we have no need to prove its falsehood? The answer: Their meaning is not that there is an essence and it has an attribute and they are actually identical as you imagine. Rather, their meaning is that His essence, exalted, produces what would be produced by both an essence and an attribute together. For example, your essence is not sufficient for the disclosure of things to you; rather, you need the attribute of knowledge that subsists in you. Unlike His essence, which does not need an attribute subsisting in it for the disclosure and manifestation of things; rather, all concepts are disclosed to Him by virtue of His essence. So His essence in this respect is the reality of knowledge. Similarly for power: His essence is effective by itself, not by an additional attribute subsisting in it, as in our essences. So it in this respect is the reality of power. On this basis, the essence and attributes are united in reality but distinct in consideration and concept. Its ultimate conclusion, when examined, is the negation of attributes while their results and fruits are obtained from the essence alone.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage responds to a hypothetical question about how an attribute could be identical with the essence, given our experience that attributes are distinct. The answer clarifies the philosophers' position: they do not claim that there is an essence and a separate attribute that are somehow one. Rather, they mean that God's essence alone suffices to produce the effects that we attribute to attributes. For example, humans need knowledge as a separate quality to know, but God knows everything by His essence alone. Similarly, God's power is His essence being effective. Thus, in reality, the essence and attributes are one; the distinction is only in our conceptual analysis. The ultimate consequence is the denial of attributes while affirming that their functions are performed by the essence itself.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the philosophers deny real attributes; the distinction is merely conceptual. The essence alone produces the effects.

Philosophers' Argument: Being Agent and Recipient
Philosophers' argument and brief reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that additional attributes would make God both agent and recipient, and the theological response rejecting that analysis.

Core Idea
If God had an attribute, He would be both its creator and its container—this is the crux of the debate.

The philosophers challenge the Ash'ari doctrine of additional attributes by pointing out a seeming contradiction: if God has a distinct attribute, He must be both the agent who creates it and the recipient in whom it subsists. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the terse reply, setting the stage for deeper debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its rhythm, then read the English translation to grasp the basic claim and response.
Notice the compact style: the argument is given in one sentence, the answer in another.
2
Identify the two positions
The philosophers present a conditional: if additional attribute, then impossible consequence. The author rejects the impossibility. Identify each side's claim.
The author says 'We do not concede its falsehood'—this is a refutation of the premise.
3
Analyze the logic step by step
Use the guided breakdown to follow the argument: why would God be both agent and recipient? What is the supposed impossibility?
The philosophers rely on the idea that one thing cannot be both the source and the recipient of the same property.
4
Reflect on the deeper debate
Consider why this argument matters for divine simplicity. The author says the discussion has already preceded—this is part of a larger contest between philosophers and theologians.

They consider this combination impossible. The answer provided by the author is succinct: we reject the claim that this is impossible, and we have already refuted it in previous discussions. This sets up a continuation of the debate.

6 min
Essence and Attributes: One Reality, Distinct Concepts
Al-Jurjani explains that attributes are not additional entities but the essence itself fulfills their functions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that God's essence and attributes are identical in reality but distinct in concept, and how this avoids anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
God's essence itself produces the effects that in creatures require separate attributes.

This passage from al-Jurjani clarifies a subtle point in Ash'ari theology. Many think that saying 'attributes are one with the essence' is a contradiction or leads to anthropomorphism. Al-Jurjani explains that it means the divine essence suffices for what attributes do in us. Let's read his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between God and creatures in the examples.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms essence (ذات), attribute (صفة), reality (الحقيقة), consideration (الاعتبار), and concept (المفهوم). Understand their roles in the argument.
3
Work through the examples
The passage gives two examples: knowledge and power. For each, note how God's essence is sufficient without a separate attribute, unlike in humans.
This illustrates the core idea.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentences state the principle: essence and attributes are united in reality, differing by consideration and concept. Understand what this implies about the attributes' reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مخيل لا يمكن أن يصدق به كما في سائر القضايا المخيلة التي يمتنع التصديق بها فلا حاجة بنا إلى الاستدلال على بطلانه قلت : ليس معنى ما ذكروه أن هناك ذاتا وله صفة وهما متحدان حقيقة كما تخيلته بل معناه أن ذاته تعالى يترتب عليه ما يترتب على ذات وصفة معاً، مثلاً ذاتك ليست كافية في انكشاف الأشياء عليك بل تحتاج في ذلك إلى صفة العلم التي تقوم بك بخلاف ذاته تعالى فإنه لا يحتاج في انكشاف الأشياء وظهورها عليه إلى صفة تقوم به، بل المفهومات بأسرها منكشفة عليه لأجل ذاته تعالى فذاته بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة العلم وكذا الحال في القدرة فإن ذاته تعالى مؤثرة بذاتها لا بصفة زائدة عليها كما في ذواتنا، فهي بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة القدرة وعلى هذا تكون الذات والصفات متحدة في الحقيقة متغايرة بالاعتبار والمفهوم، ومرجعه إذا حقق إلى نفي الصفات مع حصول نتائجها وثمراتها من الذات وحدها.
It is a fantasy that cannot be believed, like other fantastical propositions that cannot be assented to, so we have no need to argue for its falsity. I say: The meaning of what they mentioned is not that there is an essence and it has an attribute and they are actually united, as you imagined. Rather its meaning is that His essence, exalted, has the consequence of what results from both an essence and an attribute together. For example, your essence is not sufficient for the disclosure of things to you, but you need for that the attribute of knowledge that subsists in you, unlike His essence, exalted, for He does not need, for the disclosure of things and their appearance to Him, an attribute subsisting in Him. Rather all concepts are disclosed to Him because of His essence. So His essence in this respect is the reality of knowledge. Similarly, the case is with power, for His essence, exalted, is effective by itself, not by an additional attribute as in our essences. So it in this respect is the reality of power. And on this basis, the essence and the attributes are united in reality, differing by consideration and concept. And its import, when investigated, is the negation of attributes while obtaining their results and fruits from the essence alone.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari position on the relation between God's essence and attributes. It begins by dismissing a misconception: that attributes are united with the essence in a literal, anthropomorphic sense. The correct understanding is that God's essence itself fulfills the functions that in created beings require a separate attribute. For example, knowledge in humans requires a faculty, but in God, His essence is such that all concepts are disclosed to Him. Therefore, His essence is, in that respect, the reality of knowledge. Similarly, His essence is effective by itself, so it is the reality of power. Thus, the essence and attributes are one in reality (ḥaqīqa) but multiple in consideration (i'tibār) and concept (mafhūm). The net result, when investigated, is the negation of additional attributes while affirming their outcomes from the essence alone.

Key Terms
ذات
صفة
الحقيقة
الاعتبار
المفهوم
العلم
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The misunderstanding that Ash'aris believe attributes are literally two entities united with the essence, leading to anthropomorphism.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies that attributes are not additional entities; the essence itself produces their effects, so there is only one reality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how are the essence and attributes related?
A) They are two distinct entities united in one subject.
B) They are one and the same in reality but distinct in concept.
C) The attributes are separate from the essence.
D) The attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position preserves divine simplicity by identifying attributes with the essence, while still affirming that God's essence does what attributes do in creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's essence alone suffices for knowledge and power, what does that mean for your reliance on your own attributes? Do you see your own knowledge as a gift from a self-sufficient God, or as your own achievement? How does this truth change the way you seek knowledge or act with power?
?
The passage says that in creatures, essence is not sufficient; we need attributes. Where in your life are you relying on your own abilities (attributes) as if they are separate from God's giving? How can you align your understanding with the reality that all effects come from God's essence alone?
?
Are you living as if God's attributes are additional to His essence, limiting Him to human-like limitations? Reflect on a moment you felt God was 'not enough' – what does this lesson say about that feeling? What is one concrete step you can take today to trust in His essence alone?

Circularity in the Simplicity Argument
Al-Jurjani criticizes a circular argument concerning God's simplicity and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that God's simplicity implies the denial of attributes is logically circular.

Core Idea
Simplicity cannot be used to deny attributes if simplicity itself assumes that denial.

Al-Jurjani here exposes a methodological flaw in a common kalām argument. The claim that God's absolute simplicity entails having no attributes is shown to be circular: the premise of simplicity already depends on the denial of attributes. Let's read his precise critique and see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the key terms: بسيط حقيقي and دور.
2
Identify the two key concepts
The passage contrasts 'true simplicity' (بسيط حقيقي) and 'denial of attributes' (نفي الصفات). Understand each concept in context.
Simplicity here means God has no parts and no composition.
3
Grasp the circularity
Al-Jurjani says that simplicity is built upon the denial of attributes. So using simplicity to prove that denial is a vicious circle (دور). Try to state the circular reasoning in your own words.
This is the core teaching point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد تقدم بطلانه حيث بين في مباحث العلة والمعلول أن البسيط الحقيقي يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً وفيه نظر لأن كونه تعالى بسطاً حقيقاً مبني على نفي الصفات فالاحتجاج على هذا النفي بما يبتني على البساطة دور فليتأمل .
His saying: 'And its falsity has already been shown where it was explained in the investigations of cause and effect that the true simple is both recipient and agent, but there is a problem: because His being truly simple is based on the denial of attributes, so using [an argument] that is based on simplicity to prove this denial is a vicious circle. So let it be pondered.'
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage points out a methodological flaw in an argument for denying God's attributes. The argument claimed that if God had additional attributes, He would be both agent and recipient of those attributes, which is impossible for a simple being. But the notion that God is absolutely simple (basīṭ ḥaqīqī) itself presupposes that He has no attributes. Therefore, using that simplicity to prove that He has no attributes is circular reasoning (dawr). The commentator says this needs careful consideration.

Key Terms
بسيط حقيقي
دور
نفي الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simplicity is an independent, self-evident premise that logically entails the denial of attributes.
Correction: In fact, the notion that God is truly simple is itself based on the denial of attributes, so using it as a premise for that denial is circular.

Checkpoint
What is the logical problem identified in this passage?
A) The argument proves too much.
B) The argument is circular.
C) The argument is based on a false premise.
D) The argument is not valid.

Key Takeaway
The argument from simplicity to deny attributes is logically circular because simplicity presupposes the denial of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God that you have never questioned. Could it be that your belief rests on an assumption that already contains your conclusion? This week, identify one such belief and sincerely ask yourself: 'What is the foundation of this belief?' Write it down and examine it with honesty.
?
Al-Jurjani ends with 'falyutamal' — 'let it be pondered.' Is there an area of your faith where you have stopped pondering? Where you rely on familiar arguments without checking whether they stand up to scrutiny? Commit today to revisit one aspect of your understanding of God's attributes, not as a formality, but as a person who fears being in error.
?
If you were to defend your belief about God's nature to someone who challenges it, would your argument be circular? Think of a recent conversation where you defended your faith. Were your reasons independent or did they assume what you were trying to prove? Resolve to find a stronger, non-circular foundation for that belief by the end of this week.

Second Proof: The Necessity of God's Attributes and the Response
Al-Jurjani presents the second proof that God's knowledge and power are necessary, and the response distinguishing between the attribute and its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's second proof regarding the necessity of God's attributes and his response that distinguishes between the attribute itself and the effect (the attribute's name).

Core Idea
Being knowing is not a separate attribute; it is simply that knowledge subsists in the essence.

This lesson examines the second proof that God's attributes are necessary and thus do not need an external cause. Al-Jurjani presents the opponent's argument and then gives a nuanced response that clarifies the relationship between an attribute and its effect. By carefully distinguishing between the attribute (knowledge) and being described by it (being knowing), he shows that the necessity of the attribute does not mean it is independent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Note the structure: the opponent's proof begins with 'الثاني' and the response begins with 'والجواب'.
The passage includes a marginal note, so read it as a continuous unit.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that al-Jurjani distinguishes between the attribute (العلم) and the attribute's effect (العالمية). This is crucial to the response.
The deniers of states (نفاة الأحوال) reject separate states.
3
Analyse the response scenarios
The response covers two cases: first, if 'ālimiyya is not a separate thing; second, if it is granted as separate. Understand the consequence in each case.
In the first case, no separate attribute exists to be judged. In the second, its necessity cannot be self-necessity.
4
Connect to the dispute
Recognize that the core dispute is whether one necessary attribute can depend on another necessary attribute. This is what al-Jurjani mentions as 'the point of dispute'.
The opponent denies dependence; al-Jurjani allows it.
5
Reflect on the marginal note
The marginal note adds that if 'ālimiyya were self-necessary, it would have to exist, which is not claimed. Consider how this reinforces the argument.
This closes a potential loophole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: عالميته وقادريته واجبة فلا تحتاج إلى الغير والجواب أن العالمية عندنا يعني نفاة الأحوال ليست أمراً وراء قيام العلم به فيحكم بالنصب على جواب النفي عليها بأنها واجبة والحاصل أن العلم صفة قائمة بذاته وليس هناك صفة أخرى تسمى عالمية حتى يصح الحكم عليها بأنها واجبة فلا تكون محتاجة معللة بالعلم وإن سلم ثبوت العالمية فالمراد بوجوبها إن كان امتناع خلو الذات عنها فذلك لا يمنع استنادها إلى صفة أخرى واجبة أيضاً بهذا المعنى، أعني صفة العلم فإنه نفس المتنازع فيه بيننا إذ نحن نجوزه وأنتم لا تجوزونه وإن أردتم أنها أي العالمية واجبة لذاتها فبطلانه ظاهر فإن الصفة في حد ذاتها محتاجة إلى موصوفها فيمتنع اتصافها بالوجوب الذاتي.
قوله : ( فيمتنع اتصافها بالوجوب الذاتي وأيضاً العالمية إنما تكون واجبة لذاتها إذا كانت موجودة وهم لا يقولون به وإلا لزم وجود الصفة الزائدة.)
Second: His being knowing and powerful are necessary, so they do not need the other [i.e., an additional attribute]. The answer: 'Knowing-ness' ('ālimiyya) according to us—i.e., the deniers of states—is not something beyond the subsistence of knowledge in Him, so it is ruled that it is necessary. The sum is that knowledge is an attribute subsisting in His essence, and there is no other attribute called 'ālimiyya such that a judgment of necessity could be made about it. So it is not needful, not caused by knowledge. And even if the existence of 'ālimiyya is granted, if by its necessity is meant that the essence cannot be devoid of it, that does not prevent its dependence on another necessary attribute—i.e., the attribute of knowledge—for that is precisely the point of dispute between us: we allow it and you do not. And if you mean that it (the 'ālimiyya) is necessary by itself, its falsity is obvious, for the attribute in its own essence needs a substrate, so being described with self-necessity is impossible. His saying: 'So describing it with self-necessity is impossible, and also 'ālimiyya would only be necessary by itself if it existed, and they do not say that, otherwise it would entail the existence of an additional attribute.'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This is an argument about the necessity of God's attributes. The opponent claims that if attributes are necessary (wājiba), they do not require an external cause. The answer distinguishes between the attribute (knowledge) and the attribute's effect (being knowing). The 'ālimiyya (being-knowing) is not a separate entity; it is just the fact that knowledge subsists in the essence. So necessity pertains to the attribute itself, not to a separate state. If they grant the existence of 'ālimiyya as a distinct thing, its necessity cannot be self-necessity because it depends on the essence. So it must depend on the attribute of knowledge, which is what the debate is about. The marginal note adds that if 'ālimiyya were self-necessary, it would have to exist, which they do not claim, and that would imply an additional attribute.

Key Terms
عالمية
قادرية
وجوب
نفاة الأحوال
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ālimiyya (being-knowing) is a separate attribute rather than a description of the essence having knowledge.
Correction: As al-Jurjani explains, for deniers of states, 'ālimiyya is not a separate entity; it is simply the subsistence of knowledge in the essence. Even if it were separate, it cannot be self-necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the point of dispute mentioned in this passage?
A) Whether attributes are additional to essence.
B) Whether 'ālimiyya is necessary by itself.
C) Whether knowledge is an attribute.
D) Whether God is knowing.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of an attribute like being knowing does not make it an independent entity; it is simply the fact that the essence possesses that attribute, and this does not preclude dependence on another necessary attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you sometimes assume that something necessary—like your faith or a habit—stands alone without needing any support? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of confusing necessity with self-sufficiency, and how can you re-examine your own dependencies on Allah?
?
This lesson makes a subtle but powerful distinction: an attribute can be necessary without being independent. Where in your character do you claim 'I am just this way' as if it were fixed, yet you ignore that it relies on deeper causes? Will you honestly trace it back to its root—is it based on knowledge of Allah or something else?
?
If you truly grasp that every attribute depends on its subject and ultimately on the Necessary Being, how would that change the way you pray, repent, or trust? Write down one area where you have been acting as if a good quality in you stands alone, and commit to attributing it wholly to Allah's grace.

Third Proof: Perfection Attribute Implies Deficiency – The Objection and Response
Al-Jurjani presents the third proof and the response distinguishing permissible and impermissible interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that an additional perfection attribute implies God's deficiency, and how the response distinguishes between permissible and impermissible interpretations.

Core Idea
The objection fails because the attribute is for the essence, not from an external source.

This lesson presents the third proof against the Ash'ari view of attributes as additional to God's essence. The objection is that such attributes would imply that God is deficient without them and perfected by another. The answer clarifies that the attribute is for the essence itself, not from an external source.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. The passage has two parts: the proof and the answer.
Notice the concise style of the argument.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that any perfection attribute additional to the essence would make God needy and deficient.
3
Understand the distinction
The answer distinguishes between two meanings of 'perfected by another'. The permissible meaning is that the attribute is for the essence, not that the essence receives perfection from an external source.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why this distinction resolves the objection without denying the reality of attributes.
This is the point of dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث صفته تعالى صفة كمال فيلزم أن يكون ناقصاً لذاته مستكملاً بغيره والجواب إن أردتم باستكماله بالغير ثبوت صفة الكمال الزائدة على ذاته لذاته فهو جائز عندنا وهو المتنازع فيه، وإن أردتم به غيره فصوروه أولاً ثم بينوا لزومه.
قوله : ( لكن يتجه إلخ ) قال الأستاذ المحقق ذاته تعالى ليست بفاعلة لصفاته حتى يلزم أن يكون تعالى موجباً بالذات بالنسبة إليها دون غيرها، أو مختاراً إذ علة الافتقار عندهم هو الحدوث وصفاته تعالى ليست بحادثة فلا يكون لها فاعل وأنت خبير بأن هذا مشكل جدا فإن إلى تعدد الواجب إذ كل موجود لا يخلو من أن يكون وجوده عن ذاته أو عن غيره، فإذا انتفى الثاني تعين الأول فيلزم الوجوب ولهذا قال المحقق التفتازاني في شرح المقاصد إستناد الصفات عند من يثبتها ليس إلا بطريق الإيجاب. وكذا قولهم علة الاحتياج هو الحدوث دون الإمكان ينبغي أن يخص بغير الصفات نعم يرد على هذا أنه من قبيل التخصيص في القواعد العقلية كما لا يخفى .
Third: His attribute is a perfection attribute, so it would follow that He is deficient in Himself and perfected by another. The answer: If you mean by 'perfected by another' the affirmation of a perfection attribute additional to His essence for His essence, that is permissible according to us, and it is the point of dispute. If you mean something else, then describe it first, then show its consequence. His saying: 'But it is directed...' The verifier said: His essence, exalted, is not the agent of His attributes such that He would be necessitating by essence with respect to them unlike others, nor free-acting, because the cause of neediness according to them is temporality (ḥudūth), and His attributes are not temporal, so they have no agent. And you know that this is very problematic, because it implies a multiplicity of the Necessary, for every existent is either from itself or from another. If the second is negated, the first is determined, so necessity follows. Hence the verifier Taftazani said in Sharh al-Maqāsid: 'The reliance of attributes for those who affirm them is only by way of necessitation.' Similarly, their statement that the cause of need is temporality, not possibility, should be restricted to other than attributes. Indeed, it can be objected that this is a case of particularizing rational principles, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This deals with the objection that if God has an additional attribute of perfection, He would need that attribute to be perfect, implying deficiency. The answer distinguishes between the attribute being additional for the sake of the essence (which is permissible) and other interpretations. Then a lengthy marginal note discusses the issue of whether God's attributes are caused or necessitated. It examines the view that attributes are not caused because they are eternal, but then notes the problem of multiple necessary beings if attributes are existents. It cites Taftazani that attributes are necessitated. The note raises the problem of particularizing rational principles.

Key Terms
صفة كمال
ناقص
مستكمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming additional attributes implies God is perfected by an external source.
Correction: The attribute is an additional perfection for the essence itself, not from an external source.

Checkpoint
What is the permissible interpretation of 'perfected by another'?
A) God acquires perfection from an external source.
B) The attribute is a perfection additional to the essence but for the essence itself.
C) God is imperfect without attributes.
D) Attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
The objection that additional attributes imply deficiency is refuted by distinguishing between an attribute being for the essence versus from an external source.

The Causal Independence of Attributes and the Problem of Multiplicity of Necessary Beings
Al-Jurjani's marginal note discusses the causal status of attributes and the implication of multiple necessary beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's attributes are not caused, and how this leads to the problem of multiplicity of necessary beings.

Core Idea
If attributes are eternal and uncaused, they would be necessary beings, implying multiple necessary existents.

This lesson delves into a marginal note that critiques the Ash'ari position. The note argues that if attributes are not caused by God's essence, they would be necessary by themselves, leading to multiple necessary beings. It cites Taftazani and raises objections about particularizing rational principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the marginal note
Focus on the part starting from 'قوله : ( لكن يتجه إلخ )'. Read the Arabic and translation.
This is a dense discussion.
2
Understand the first claim
The verifier argues that God is not the agent of His attributes because the cause of neediness is temporality, and attributes are eternal.
3
Identify the problem
This implies that attributes are necessary by themselves, leading to multiplicity of necessary beings.
Think about the logical consequence.
4
Examine Taftazani's view
He says attributes are necessitated (by the essence), not free.
5
Reflect on the objection
The note itself says this is problematic and ends with a concern about particularizing rational principles.
This shows the complexity of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث صفته تعالى صفة كمال فيلزم أن يكون ناقصاً لذاته مستكملاً بغيره والجواب إن أردتم باستكماله بالغير ثبوت صفة الكمال الزائدة على ذاته لذاته فهو جائز عندنا وهو المتنازع فيه، وإن أردتم به غيره فصوروه أولاً ثم بينوا لزومه.
قوله : ( لكن يتجه إلخ ) قال الأستاذ المحقق ذاته تعالى ليست بفاعلة لصفاته حتى يلزم أن يكون تعالى موجباً بالذات بالنسبة إليها دون غيرها، أو مختاراً إذ علة الافتقار عندهم هو الحدوث وصفاته تعالى ليست بحادثة فلا يكون لها فاعل وأنت خبير بأن هذا مشكل جدا فإن إلى تعدد الواجب إذ كل موجود لا يخلو من أن يكون وجوده عن ذاته أو عن غيره، فإذا انتفى الثاني تعين الأول فيلزم الوجوب ولهذا قال المحقق التفتازاني في شرح المقاصد إستناد الصفات عند من يثبتها ليس إلا بطريق الإيجاب. وكذا قولهم علة الاحتياج هو الحدوث دون الإمكان ينبغي أن يخص بغير الصفات نعم يرد على هذا أنه من قبيل التخصيص في القواعد العقلية كما لا يخفى .
Third: His attribute is a perfection attribute, so it would follow that He is deficient in Himself and perfected by another. The answer: If you mean by 'perfected by another' the affirmation of a perfection attribute additional to His essence for His essence, that is permissible according to us, and it is the point of dispute. If you mean something else, then describe it first, then show its consequence. His saying: 'But it is directed...' The verifier said: His essence, exalted, is not the agent of His attributes such that He would be necessitating by essence with respect to them unlike others, nor free-acting, because the cause of neediness according to them is temporality (ḥudūth), and His attributes are not temporal, so they have no agent. And you know that this is very problematic, because it implies a multiplicity of the Necessary, for every existent is either from itself or from another. If the second is negated, the first is determined, so necessity follows. Hence the verifier Taftazani said in Sharh al-Maqāsid: 'The reliance of attributes for those who affirm them is only by way of necessitation.' Similarly, their statement that the cause of need is temporality, not possibility, should be restricted to other than attributes. Indeed, it can be objected that this is a case of particularizing rational principles, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This deals with the objection that if God has an additional attribute of perfection, He would need that attribute to be perfect, implying deficiency. The answer distinguishes between the attribute being additional for the sake of the essence (which is permissible) and other interpretations. Then a lengthy marginal note discusses the issue of whether God's attributes are caused or necessitated. It examines the view that attributes are not caused because they are eternal, but then notes the problem of multiple necessary beings if attributes are existents. It cites Taftazani that attributes are necessitated. The note raises the problem of particularizing rational principles.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
تعدد الواجب
حدوث
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is not the agent of His attributes, then attributes are independent necessary beings.
Correction: Attributes are necessitated by the essence, not independent, as Taftazani argues.

Checkpoint
Why does the note say that the argument that attributes have no agent implies multiplicity of the Necessary?
A) Because attributes would be contingent.
B) Because every existent must be either from itself or another, and negating the other leaves self-sufficiency and necessity.
C) Because attributes are temporal.
D) Because God's essence is simple.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of attributes leads to the problem of multiple necessary beings unless their existence is necessitated by the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever subconsciously imagined multiple 'powers' in the heavens? This passage forces you to examine whether your heart affirms absolute tawḥīd or merely pays lip service to it. Commit to studying how the early scholars defended divine unity against polytheistic tendencies.
?
The argument leads to a logical dilemma: if attributes are uncaused, they become necessary beings alongside God. Does this make you uncomfortable? Resolve to seek knowledge with humility and a seeking heart.
?
The note ends by pointing out that restricting rational principles is a form of particularization. Are there areas in your life where you have made exceptions to God's commands based on your own 'rational principles'? Repent and realign your priorities with the divine revelation, trusting that God's wisdom transcends human reason.

Definition of Qadir and Disagreement with Philosophers
Al-Iji defines qadir and contrasts the Mutakallimun view with the philosophers' view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of 'Qadir' as used in kalam, the disagreement between the Mutakallimun and the philosophers regarding God's power, and the agreed conditional meaning.

Core Idea
Qadir means God can create or not create; philosophers deny this but still affirm power in a conditional sense.

This lesson introduces a foundational term in Islamic theology: 'Qadir' (powerful). Al-Iji first defines it from the perspective of the Mutakallimun, showing God's absolute freedom. He then contrasts this with the philosophers' view that God's creation is necessary, and finally presents a second meaning agreed upon by both groups. Understanding this distinction is crucial for the debates on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Find the primary definition
Identify the definition of Qadir given by the Mutakallimun: it is valid for God to create the world and to leave it, neither being necessary for His essence.
3
Contrast with philosophers
Notice that the philosophers deny this definition because they believe creation is a necessary concomitant of God's essence, and instead affirm necessary causation (ijab).
4
Understand the agreed meaning
Both groups agree on a second meaning: Qadir means that if He wills He acts, if He does not will He does not act. But the philosophers add that His will itself is necessary.
The passage ends with a conditional structure reflecting this nuance.
5
Reflect on the conditional
Consider the statement: 'the antecedent of the first conditional is necessarily true, the antecedent of the second is impossible, and both conditionals are true.' This captures the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( في قدرته وفيه بحثان الأول في أنه تعالى قادر أي يصح منه إيجاد العالم وتركه فليس شيء منهما لازماً لذاته بحيث يستحيل انفكاكه عنه، وإلى هذا ذهب المليون كلهم وأما الفلاسفة فإنهم قالوا إيجاده للعالم على النظام الواقع من لوازم ذاته فيمتنع خلوه عنه فأنكروا القدرة بالمعنى المذكور لاعتقادهم أنه نقصان، وأثبتوا له الإيجاب زعماً منهم أن الكمال التام وأما كونه تعالى قادراً بمعنى إن شاء فعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل فهو متفق عليه بين الفريقين، إلا أن الحكماء ذهبوا إلى أن مشيئة الفعل الذي هو الفيض والجود لازمة لذاته كلزوم العلم وسائر الصفات الكمالية له فيستحيل الانفكاك بينهما، فمقدم الشرطية الأولى واجب صدقه ومقدم الثانية ممتنع الصدق وكلتا الشرطيتين صادقتان في حق الباري سبحانه وتعالى
Concerning His power, and in it there are two investigations. The first: that He, exalted, is Qadir (powerful), meaning that it is valid for Him to create the world and to leave it, so neither of them is necessary for His essence such that its separation from Him is impossible. This is the view of all the Milla [people]. As for the philosophers, they said that His creation of the world in the actual order is among the concomitants of His essence, so it is impossible for Him to be devoid of it. Thus they denied power in the mentioned sense, believing it to be a deficiency, and they affirmed for Him necessary causation (ijab), claiming that [this] is complete perfection. As for His being powerful in the sense that if He wills He acts and if He does not will He does not act, this is agreed upon between the two groups, except that the wise ones (al-hukama') held that the will of the act, which is the effusion and generosity, is necessary for His essence like the necessity of knowledge and the rest of the attributes of perfection for Him, so separation between them is impossible. Thus the antecedent of the first conditional is necessarily true, and the antecedent of the second is impossible of truth, and both conditionals are true concerning the Exalted.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'Qadir' (powerful) as used in kalam. It begins by stating that God is Qadir, meaning He can create the world or leave it—neither is necessary for His essence. The author then contrasts this with the philosophers' view: they say the world's existence in its actual order is a necessary concomitant of God's essence, so He cannot be without it. They therefore deny power in the first sense because they think it implies a deficiency, and instead affirm that He is a necessary cause (mujib bi al-dhat). However, both groups agree on a second meaning: God is Qadir in the sense that if He wills He acts, if He does not will He does not act. Yet the philosophers (al-hukama') claim that His will itself is necessary, like knowledge and other attributes, so it is impossible for His will to be separated from the act. The passage ends by noting a conditional structure: one conditional has a true antecedent, another an impossible antecedent, but both conditionals are true of God. This sets up the proof that follows.

قدرة
إيجاب
مليون
فلاسفة
حكماء
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny God's power entirely.
Correction: The philosophers only deny the first definition of Qadir (freedom to create or not). They affirm a second, conditional meaning and also affirm God's necessary causation (ijab).

Checkpoint

A) That God necessarily creates the world.
B) That God can create or leave the world, neither being necessary for His essence.
C) That God acts only through necessary causation.
D) That God's will is identical to His essence.

Key Takeaway
Qadir has two definitions: the primary Mutakallimun sense of absolute freedom, and a conditional sense agreed upon with philosophers, who nevertheless maintain that God's will is necessary.

The Two Relations of Divine Power
Al-Jurjani distinguishes the eternal enabling relation from the temporal effect relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two relations of divine power—the eternal non-effectual relation and the temporal effectual relation—and the key objection and response concerning the eternal nature of the effectual relation.

Core Idea
Divine power has two relations: one that enables and is eternal, and one that effects and is temporal.

Al-Jurjani delves into the precise nature of divine power (qudra) by distinguishing two distinct ways it relates to its objects. Understanding these relations is crucial for grasping how God's eternal power interacts with temporal creation without implying change in God. This passage presents the two relations, addresses a potential objection about pre-eternity, and clarifies the temporal aspect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the definitions of the two relations.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the two relations
The passage first defines a 'non-effectual relation' (tacalluq ma'nawi) and then an 'effectual relation'. Note the characteristics: eternal vs. temporal, general vs. specific.
3
Study the objection and response
An objection suggests that if the effectual relation is pre-eternal, it contradicts mutakallimun principles. Trace the response explaining why the relation must be temporal.
The key point: the pre-eternal relation alone is insufficient; the specific time is needed.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction protects God's transcendence and explains the temporality of events without making God subject to change.
This connects to broader Aqeedah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في قدرته تعالى إعلم أن القدرة صفة تؤثر في المقدور ولها به تعلقان تعلق معنوي لا يترتب عليه وجود المقدور بل يمكن القادر من إيجاده وتركه ولا شك أن هذا التعلق عام لكل ممكن لازم للقدرة قديم بقدمها ونسبته إلى الضدين على السواء وتعلق آخر يترتب عليه ذلك وهذا التعلق حادث كما صرح به الشارح في مباحث القدرة وكلام المقاصد يشعر بجواز قدمه على معنى أن القدرة تعلقت في الأزل بوجود المقدور فيما لا يزال، وكلام المصنف والشارح أيضاً فيما سيأتي يشعر به فإن قلت القول بقدم التعلق التأثير يفضي إلى القول بتوقف صدور خصوصيات الحوادث على شروط أو استعدادات تحصل في المواد والألم يتخلف عن ذلك التعلق فلا يلائم أصول المتكلمين قلت: إنما لا يلائمها إذ لم يكف في وقوع المقدور تعلق القدرة في وقت معين فيما لا يزال وحصول ذلك الوقت والله أعلم
His saying: 'Concerning His power, exalted: Know that power is an attribute that has effect in the object of power, and it has two relations with it: a non-effectual relation (tacalluq ma'nawi) by which the existence of the object of power does not follow, but rather it enables the powerful one to create it and leave it. There is no doubt that this relation is general to every possible, necessary for power, eternal with its eternity, and its relation to opposites is equal. And there is another relation by which [the existence] follows, and this relation is temporal, as the commentator (al-Sharh) stated in the discussions of power. And the discourse of the objectives (al-Maqasid) suggests the permissibility of its pre-eternity, in the sense that power was related in pre-eternity to the existence of the object of power in the future. And the discourse of the author and the commentator also in what follows suggests it. If you say: The statement of the pre-eternity of the effectual relation leads to the statement that the occurrence of particularities of events depends on conditions or predispositions that occur in matters, and the pain would be deferred from that relation, so it does not conform to the principles of the mutakallimun. I say: It only does not conform because it is not sufficient for the occurrence of the object of power—the relation of power at a specific time in the future and the occurrence of that time. And God knows best.'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of divine power (qudra) and its two relations to the objects of power (maqdur). First, there is a non-effectual relation (tacalluq ma'nawi) that does not bring about the object's existence but enables the powerful agent to create or leave it. This relation is general to all possibles, eternal, and equal with respect to opposites. Second, there is an effectual relation that brings about the object's existence, and this relation is temporal—it occurs at the moment the object is created. The commentator notes that some texts suggest this relation might be pre-eternal, meaning power was related in pre-eternity to the future existence of the object. However, an objection is raised: if the effectual relation is pre-eternal, then the occurrence of particular events would depend only on that relation without needing conditions or predispositions, which contradicts the mutakallimun's principles. The answer is that the pre-eternal relation alone is not sufficient; the occurrence also requires the arrival of the specific time. So the relation is at a specific future time, not merely pre-eternal.

Key Terms
قدرة
مقدور
تعلق
تعلق معنوي
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The eternal relation of divine power directly determines the actual existence of all things from eternity.
Correction: The first relation (tacalluq ma'nawi) is only enabling, not effectual; it does not bring about existence. The effectual relation is temporal and occurs at the time of creation.

Checkpoint
What is the nature of the first relation (tacalluq ma'nawi) of divine power?
A) It brings about the existence of the object.
B) It is temporal and specific to each event.
C) It enables the powerful one to create or leave, without producing existence itself.
D) It is the same as the effectual relation.

Key Takeaway
Divine power has two relations: an eternal enabling relation that does not cause existence, and a temporal effectual relation that brings about existence at the appointed time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you ever act as if your own power to choose is independent of God's enabling, forgetting that your ability to even intend an action is from His eternal enabling relation? How does this lesson humble your sense of self-sufficiency?
?

?
This lesson distinguishes between God's eternal capacity and its temporal execution. In what area of your life are you trying to force the effect when the enabling relation is already there but the time has not come? How can you align your expectations with this theological truth?

The First Proof: Impossibility of Infinite Regress of Accidents
First proof explaining why infinite regress of accidents in God's essence is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite regress of successive accidents in God's essence is impossible according to the first proof, and how it relies on the principle of the complete cause.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of conditions prevents a necessary cause from producing a contingent effect.

This lesson presents the first proof from the text, which argues that an infinite regress of accidentally occurring attributes in God's essence is impossible. The proof uses the concept of the 'complete cause' (al-mūjib al-tāmm) to show that such a regress would require an endless chain of conditions, making the effect impossible. You will see how this reasoning fits into the broader argument for the contingency of created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argumentative rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Understand the conditional hypothesis
The passage begins by supposing that an infinite regress of successive accidents in God's essence is possible. Identify what consequence is derived from this supposition.
The consequence involves a contingent event requiring an infinite chain of conditions.
3
Examine the reasoning on the complete cause
Focus on the statement about the 'complete cause' (al-mūjib al-tāmm). Why does the argument say that the effect of an eternal necessitating cause cannot be contingent without infinite regress? What does 'without condition or with an eternal condition' imply?
The complete cause necessitates its effect immediately; any delay would require an additional condition.
4
See how the proof is completed
The proof references two established premises: (1) everything other than God's essence and attributes is contingent, and (2) infinite regress of accidents in His essence is impossible. How do these premises together refute the initial hypothesis?
The premises show that the required infinite chain is impossible, so the original hypothesis is false.
5
Reflect on the key principle
Summarize the core idea: an infinite regress would prevent any effect from being contingent because it would require an endless series of conditions, violating the necessity of the complete cause.
This principle is central to many arguments in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متعاقبة لا نهاية لها بذاته إذ لو جاز ذلك لأمكن أن يصدر عنه مع كونه موجباً حادث مشروط بصفة حادثة قائمة بذاته مشروطة بصفة أخرى وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، وإذا ثبت حدوث ما سوى ذاته وصفاته وثبت أيضاً إستحالة قيام الصفات المتعاقبة إلى ما لا نهاية له بذاته تم الاستدلال المذكور بهذا الطريق لأن أثر الموجب القديم لا يكون حادثا بلا تسلسل الحوادث فإن الصادر عنه بلا شرط أو بشرط قد قدیم قطعاً لامتناع التخلف عن الموجب التام كما عرفت.
Successive with no end in His essence, for if that were possible, then it would be possible for something to proceed from Him while He is a necessitating cause, a contingent event conditioned by an accidentally occurring attribute subsisting in His essence, conditioned by another attribute, and so on to infinity. And if the contingency of everything other than His essence and attributes is established, and also the impossibility of the subsistence of successive attributes to infinity in His essence is established, then the aforementioned proof is completed by this path, because the effect of the eternal necessitating cause cannot be contingent without an infinite regress of events. For what proceeds from Him without condition or with an eternal condition is necessarily eternal, due to the impossibility of the effect's delay from the complete cause, as you have known.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ṭarīq al-awwal) for the impossibility of an infinite regress of accidents (ṣifāt mutaʿāqiba) in God's essence. The argument begins by stating that if such a regress were possible, then a contingent event could issue from God as a necessitating cause, but only if it is conditioned by an accident that is itself contingent and conditioned by another accident, leading to an infinite chain. However, it has already been established that everything other than God's essence and attributes is contingent, and that an infinite regress of accidents in His essence is impossible. Therefore, the proof is complete: because the effect of an eternal necessitating cause cannot be contingent without an infinite regress of events. Anything that proceeds from Him either without condition or with an eternal condition is necessarily eternal, since the effect cannot be delayed from a complete cause (al-mūjib al-tāmm).

Key Terms
التعاقب
الموجب التام
التسلسل
صفة حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this proof assumes that all effects must be simultaneous with their causes, but it relies on the principle of the complete cause (al-mūjib al-tāmm) which necessitates the effect without delay.
Correction: The proof does not require simultaneity; it uses the stronger condition that the complete cause necessitates its effect immediately, so any delay would require an additional condition.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof establish as impossible in God's essence?
A) The existence of any attributes
B) An infinite regress of successive accidents
C) The contingency of all things
D) The delay of effect from cause

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that an infinite regress of successive attributes in God's essence is impossible because it would prevent the complete cause from producing a contingent effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Objection Against God's Power and the Answer
Objection and answer about God's power requiring a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's power would require an infinite regress of preponderators and how the Ash'ari answer resolves it by affirming that the attachment of power is by essence.

Core Idea
God's power attaches to its objects by essence, not by an external motive.

This passage presents a powerful objection against God being a free agent: if God's power relates equally to opposites, then any specification would require an external preponderator, leading to an infinite regress. The Ash'ari answer, however, distinguishes between the power's attachment and the will's motive, using the examples of the fleeing and the thirsty to show that free choice does not need a determining cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the objection presents two horns.
The passage begins with 'لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً' setting up the objection.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection argues that the attachment of power to one opposite is either by essence (without a preponderator) or by an external preponderator. Identify each horn and its consequences.
The first horn leads to the independence of the possible and eternity of effect; the second leads to infinite regress.
3
Understand the answer's choice
The answer chooses the first horn but clarifies that attachment by essence does not imply independence from a preponderator; rather, the will of the free agent does not require an external motive.
The answer says 'نختار أن تعلقها بأحد المقدورين إنما هو بذاتها لا بأمر خارج'.
4
Relate to the examples of the fleeing and thirsty
The answer refers to the two ways of the fleeing and the thirsty living being (طريقي الهارب وقد حي العطشان) to illustrate that free choice does not require a motive beyond the agent's essence.
These examples demonstrate that a free agent can choose without an external cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً إذ تعلق القدرة منه بأحد الضدين المقدورين له، كتخصيص الجسم بشكل معين ولون مخصوص مثلا دون ما عداه الأشكال والألوان إما لذاتها بلا مرجح وداع فيستغني الممكن عن المرجح، لأن نسبة ذات القدرة إلى الضدين على السوية كما اعترف به القائل بقادريته وإنه يسد باب إثبات الصانع، إذ يجوز حينئذ أن يترجح وجود الممكن على عدمه من غير مرجح وأيضاً يلزم قدم الأثر لأن المؤثر حينئذ مستجمع لشرائط التأثير لأن الواجب أزلي وكذا قدرته وتعلقها فلا يجوز تخلف الأثر عنه وهو باطل، لأن أثر القادر حادث اتفاقاً وخصوصاً على رأيكم وإما لا لذاتها فيحتاج تعلقها به إلى مرجح من خارج، ومع المرجح لا يجب الفعل وإلا لزم الإيجاب بل كان جائزا هو وضده أيضا فيحتاج إلى مرجح آخر ويلزم التسلسل في المرجحات والجواب نختار أن تعلقها بأحد المقدورين إنما هو بذاتها لا بأمر خارج وليس يحتاج تعلق إرادة المختار بأحد مقدوريه إلى داع كما بينا في طريقي الهارب وقد حي العطشان قولكم
It is not permissible for Him to be powerful, for the attachment of power from Him to one of the two opposites that are within His power—such as the specification of a body with a particular shape and a particular color, for example, to the exclusion of other shapes and colors—is either by its essence without a preponderator (murajjiḥ) and motive (dāʿī), so the possible (al-mumkin) becomes independent of the preponderator, because the relation of the essence of power to the two opposites is equal, as the proponent of His powerfulness admits; and that closes the door to proving the Maker, for then it would be permissible for the existence of the possible to be preferred over its non-existence without a preponderator; and also it necessitates the eternity of the effect, because the cause then possesses all conditions of effectiveness, for the Necessary (al-wājib) is eternal, as is His power and its attachment, so the effect's failure from Him is not permissible—yet this is false, because the effect of the powerful agent is temporal by agreement, especially in your view. Or it is not by its essence, so its attachment to it needs an external preponderator, and with the preponderator the act is not necessitated—otherwise necessitation would follow—rather, it and its opposite are both possible, so it needs another preponderator, leading to an infinite regress of preponderators. And the answer: we choose that its attachment to one of the two objects of power is by its essence, not by an external matter, and the attachment of the will of the free agent to one of its objects of power does not need a motive, as we have demonstrated in the two ways of the fleeing (al-hārib) and the thirsty living being (ḥay al-ʿaṭshān), your saying...
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against God being a powerful agent (qādir) and the answer. The objection argues that if God's power attaches to one of two opposites (like specific shape) either by its essence without a preponderator, then the possible becomes independent of a preponderator, which would close the door to proving the Creator and lead to the eternity of the effect—both false. Alternatively, if the attachment is not by essence, then it needs an external preponderator, but with a preponderator the act is not necessitated, so it would still be possible for the opposite to occur, requiring another preponderator, leading to infinite regress. The answer chooses the first horn: the attachment is by essence, not needing an external motive, and the will of the free agent does not require a motive. The commentator refers to the example of the fleeing (al-hārib) and the thirsty (al-ʿaṭshān) to illustrate that free choice does not require a motive beyond the agent's essence.

Key Terms
قادر
تعلق القدرة
الضدين
مرجح
داع
إرادة المختار
طريقي الهارب وقد حي العطشان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies that God has a will or that His will is free.
Correction: The answer does not deny God's will; it denies the need for an external motive for the will's attachment. The will acts by its essence without requiring a preponderator.

Checkpoint
In the answer, why does the attachment of power to one opposite not require an external preponderator?
A) Because God's power is infinite
B) Because the attachment is by its essence, not by an external cause
C) Because the opposite does not exist
D) Because the preponderator is God Himself

Key Takeaway
God's power attaches to one of two opposites by its essence, not by an external preponderator, thus avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you had to choose between two equally good options, with no external reason to prefer one over the other. Did you attribute that choice to your own free will? How does that experience help you understand the nature of God's will, as explained in this lesson? What does it require of you to trust that God's choices are not arbitrary but spring from His perfect will?
?
The objection claims that if God's power attaches by essence, then the effect would be eternal. But the answer says no because the will attaches without a motive. Does this resolution satisfy you? Or do you find yourself still uneasy about the idea that God can choose without a reason? Examine your heart: do you demand that God's actions conform to human notions of causation? What would it mean to surrender that demand?
?
In this passage, the escape from infinite regress is to ground choice in the essence of the will itself. Where in your own life do you seek an ultimate reason—a 'preponderator'—for your decisions, whether in your career, relationships, or faith? Are you willing to accept that some choices are simply from your will, mirroring the divine? How does that affect your accountability before God?

Divine Power's Self-Determined Choice
Al-Iji presents and refutes the two-horned objection against divine power's self-determined choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against divine power's self-determined choice and how al-Iji refutes it by distinguishing between preference without a motive and preference without an agent.

Core Idea
Divine power's choice needs no external motive—it is self-determined, not self-sufficient.

This passage presents a classic objection: if God's power attaches to one of two alternatives, either by its own essence or not. Both horns seem problematic. Al-Iji answers by clarifying that 'by essence' means without external motive, not that the possible becomes independent of a determinant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's two horns
Carefully read the passage up to 'ويلزم التسلسل في المرجحات'. Identify the two horns of the dilemma and note the consequences drawn from each.
The first horn claims independence of the possible; the second leads to infinite regress.
2
Identify the core dilemma
The objection tries to show that either way, divine power cannot be both free and effective. Ask yourself: what assumption does the objection rely on?
The assumption is that 'by essence' would make the possible pointless.
3
Examine the answer's distinction
Read al-Iji's answer starting from 'والجواب نختار...'. Notice how he distinguishes between tarjīḥ (preference) without a motive and tarjīḥ without any agent at all.
This is the crucial nuance: the possible still needs a determinant (the agent), but the agent's choice need not be caused by an external motive.
4
Reflect on the refutation of the alternative
Al-Iji also addresses those who take the second horn and claim 'preference without necessity' is enough. Understand why he deems that insufficient.
The key point: mere preference short of necessity is insufficient for the emanation of a possible from an agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً إذ تعلق القدرة منه بأحد الضدين المقدورين له، كتخصيص الجسم بشكل معين ولون مخصوص مثلاً دون ما عداه من الأشكال والألوان إما لذاتها بلا مرجح وداع فيستغني الممكن عن المرجح، لأن نسبة ذات القدرة إلى الضدين على السوية كما اعترف به القائل بقادريته، وإنه يسد باب إثبات الصانع، إذ يجوز حينئذ أن يترجح وجود الممكن على عدمه من غير مرجح. وأيضاً يلزم قدم الأثر لأن المؤثر حينئذ مستجمع لشرائط التأثير لأن الواجب أزلي وكذا قدرته وتعلقها فلا يجوز تخلف الأثر عنه وهو باطل، لأن أثر القادر حادث اتفاقاً وخصوصاً على رأيكم. وإما لا لذاتها فيحتاج تعلقها به إلى مرجح من خارج، ومع المرجح لا يجب الفعل وإلا لزم الإيجاب بل كان جائزاً هو وضده أيضاً فيحتاج إلى مرجح آخر ويلزم التسلسل في المرجحات. والجواب نختار أن تعلقها بأحد المقدورين إنما هو بذاتها لا بأمر خارج، وليس يحتاج تعلق إرادة المختار بأحد مقدوريه إلى داع كما بينا في طريقي الهارب والعطشان. قوله: ( فيستغني الممكن عن المرجح ) قلنا: لا يلزم من ترجيح القادر لأحد مقدوريه على الآخر بلا مرجح وداع ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن في حد ذاته غير المرجح المؤثر فيه، إذ بينهما بون بعيد، كما أشار إليه بقوله: وبالجملة فالترجيح الصادر عن مؤثر قادر بلا مرجح أي بلا داعية غير الترجيح بلا مرجح أي بلا مؤثر أصلاً مغايرة ظاهرة، ولا يلزم من صحته صحته أي من صحة الأول صحة الثاني. ألا ترى أن بديهة العقل شاهدة بامتناع الثاني بلا توقف، ولذلك لم يذهب إلى صحته أحد العقلاء ولا يشهد كذلك بامتناع الأول، ومن ثمة ترى جمعاً يجوزونه. وربما يختار أن تعلقها لا لذاتها، ويقال الفعل مع الداعي أولى بالوقوع ولا ينتهي إلى الوجوب فلا يلزم الإيجاب ولا يحتاج أيضاً إلى مرجح آخر ليتسلسل، وقد عرفت ضعفه بما مر من أن الأولوية التي لم تنته إلى حد الوجوب غير كافية في صدور الممكن عن المؤثر.
It is not permissible that He be powerful, for the attachment of power to one of the two opposites that are His objects of power, like specifying a body with a specific shape and specific color, for example, to the exclusion of other shapes and colors, is either [1] by its essence without any determinant or motive, then the possible would be independent of the determinant, because the relation of the essence of power to the two opposites is equal, as admitted by the one claiming His power, and that would block the door of proving the Creator, for then it would be permissible for the existence of the possible to be preferred over its non-existence without a determinant. Also, it would entail the eternity of the effect, because the agent would then possess all conditions of effect, since the Necessary is eternal, and likewise His power and its attachment, so the effect cannot be delayed from Him, and that is false because the effect of the powerful is temporally originated by agreement, especially according to your view. [2] Or it is not by its essence, then its attachment to it requires an external determinant, and with the determinant the act is not necessary, otherwise it would entail necessitation; rather it and its opposite are both permissible, so it needs another determinant, and there would be an infinite regress of determinants. The answer: We choose that its attachment to one of the two objects of power is by its essence, not by an external matter, and the attachment of the will of the chooser to one of its two objects of power does not need a motive, as we explained in the example of the two fleeing [from a lion] and the thirsty meeting water. He said: (Then the possible would be independent of the determinant), we reply: The preferring by the powerful of one of his two objects of power over the other without a determinant or motive does not entail the preference of one side of the possible in its own essence without the determinant that effects it, for there is a great difference between them, as he indicated by saying: In short, the preference proceeding from an effective powerful without a determinant – i.e., without a motive – is distinct from the preference without any effective agent at all, and the soundness of the first does not entail the soundness of the second. Do you not see that the intellect's intuition testifies to the impossibility of the second without hesitation, and therefore no rational person has gone to its soundness, nor does it similarly testify to the impossibility of the first, hence you see many permitting it. And perhaps one chooses that its attachment is not by its essence, and says: The act with the motive is more worthy of occurrence and does not lead to necessity, so necessitation does not follow, nor does it need another determinant to cause regress. And you have known its weakness from what has passed, that preference which does not reach the level of necessity is insufficient for the emanation of the possible from the effective agent.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objection poses a dilemma: either the divine power's attachment to one of two alternatives is by its own essence (without external determinant) or it is not. If it is by essence, then the possible becomes independent of any determinant, which would deny the need for a Creator and also entail that the effect (creation) would be eternal, contradicting its temporality. If it is not by essence, then an external determinant is needed; but with that determinant, the action is not necessary (otherwise necessitation), so a further determinant would be needed ad infinitum. The answer chooses the first horn but clarifies what 'by essence' means: it does not mean that the possible becomes self-determinate, but that the powerful acts without an external motive. The distinction is between tarjīḥ (preference) without a motive (which is permissible for a powerful agent) and tarjīḥ without any agent at all (which is impossible). The author then refutes a possible alternative (taking the second horn) by noting that mere preference (awlawiyya) short of necessity is insufficient for the emanation of a possible effect from an agent.

مرجح

ترجيح
إيجاب
تسلسل
ذات القدرة
أولوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'attachment by essence' means the possible is self-sufficient and needs no determinant at all.
Correction: It only means the agent's choice is not caused by an external motive; the possible still requires the agent as determinant.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what does it mean for the divine power's attachment to be 'by its essence'?
A) That the possible is independent of any determinant.
B) That the agent acts without an external motive, not that the possible needs no determinant.
C) That the power necessitates the effect, so the effect is eternal.
D) That the power requires an external determinant to choose one alternative.

Key Takeaway
Divine power's attachment to one opposite is self-determined (without external motive), but this does not make the possible independent of the agent; it only means the agent's choice is free from external causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you search for external reasons to justify your choice, or do you trust that your will—even if self-determined—can be wise? How does God's self-determined choice challenge your reliance on external motives for your own actions?
?
Have you ever felt that God 'owed' you an explanation for a trial? How does knowing that His power chooses without external cause—yet not arbitrarily—reshape your response to His decrees? What will you do the next time you are tempted to demand a reason from Him?
?
Consider a time you judged someone else's decision as 'arbitrary' because you couldn't see their motive. How does this lesson's distinction between 'preference without external motive' and 'preference without any agent' apply to your own judgments of others? Will you now pause before labeling someone's choice as groundless?
